But before that, we need to solve problem in general. The problem asks: "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following: for all positive integers a and b with a+b ≤ n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines; and exactly k of the n lines are sunny." So we need to characterize all possible k for a given n≥3.

Observations: The points to cover are lattice points in a triangular region: (a,b) with a,b≥1 integers, a+b ≤ n+1. So there are N = n(n+1)/2 points.

We have n lines. Each line can cover many points. We need to cover all points.

We need to count how many of these lines are "sunny" i.e., not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0. So we need to understand constraints on k.

Potential approach: Use combinatorial geometry, covering problems. Possibly the answer is all k except k=2? But n=3 gave k=0,1,3. n=4 gave k=0,1,3. So k=2 is missing, but also k=4 is missing for n=4. So maybe for each n, the possible k are 0,1, and all k from n-2 to n? But n=3, n-2=1, so k=1, and n=3 is 3, so that would give 0,1,3. For n=4, n-2=2, but k=2 is missing, so not that.

Maybe we need to consider the concept of "sunny lines" and "non-sunny lines". The non-sunny lines are those with slopes 0, ∞, or -1. So they are axis-aligned or anti-diagonal. These lines can cover many points if they pass through many lattice points. The lines with slope 0 (horizontal) cover points with a fixed y. For covering the triangle, the best horizontal line is y=1 covering n points (since a+b≤n+1, a≥1, so if y=1, then a ≤ n). Similarly vertical x=1 covers n points. The anti-diagonal x+y=c for c from 2 to n+1 covers c-1 points. So the maximum number of points on a non-sunny line is n (for c=2? Actually x+y=2 covers 1 point; x+y=n+1 covers n points). So the best non-sunny line covers n points.

We need to cover N points with n lines. This is reminiscent of covering a triangular lattice with lines. Possibly the minimal number of lines needed to cover all points is something like? Actually (2,3) is in x=2, so covered; missing (3,2) and (2,2)? (2,2) covered; (3,2) not covered. So missing (3,2). So not all. Could we use y=1 (4), x+y=5 (4), and x=2 (3)? So distinct: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2) plus (2,2) from x=2. Also (2,3) already, (2,2) new. So total: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(2,2) = 8 points. And the problem asks: for a given n, we need to use exactly n lines. So we are allowed to use exactly n lines. We want to know possible k (number of sunny lines) among those n lines.

Thus we need to find configurations of n lines that cover all points. Possibly the only possible configurations are those where the non-sunny lines are "full" lines that cover many points, and the sunny lines cover the rest. Maybe we need to analyze the structure of coverings. Possibly the covering condition forces that the lines must be the three families in a certain way. Let's try to understand necessary conditions.

Let the set of lines be L1,...,Ln. For each point (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1, there exists a line Li such that (a,b) ∈ Li.

We want to maximize/minimize number of sunny lines.

Think of each line as covering a set of points. The triangle is "convex" in lattice. Possibly we can use lines that are "sides" of the triangle. The points lie on a grid. The lines x=1, y=1, x+y=n+1 are "boundary" lines. Those are non-sunny. They cover many points. Could we cover all points using only these three lines? For n=3, union of x=1,y=1,x+y=4 covers all 6 points? Actually we saw x=1 covers (1,1)-(1,4) but (1,4) not in set for n=3 (sum5>4). Probably not. So maybe minimal number of lines needed to cover all points is n. For n=3, 3 lines suffice; for n=4, 4 lines suffice; for n=5, likely 5 lines suffice. So we have n lines, which is minimal. So we need to cover all points with exactly n lines. That imposes constraints: each line must cover many points; maybe the covering is essentially a "partition" of the triangle into n lines each covering a set of points that are "independent" in some sense.

Observation: The number of points is N = n(n+1)/2. If we have n lines, the average number of points per line is N/n = (n+1)/2. So average coverage is about half of n. The maximum coverage by a line is n (if it's a horizontal or vertical or anti-diagonal line that goes through the whole triangle). But if a line covers n points, it might be x=1 (covers a=1..n) or y=1, or x+y=n+1. So those are the best.

We need to cover all points. So likely the covering must include lines that cover the "boundary" points. Perhaps we can classify possible coverings based on the number of non-sunny lines. Since non-sunny lines are the only ones that can cover many points (up to n). Sunny lines cover at most 2 points? Actually we need to check: Could a sunny line cover 3 points? For larger n, we can have many points on a line with slope 1 (y=x) as long as a+b≤n+1, so a=b gives 2a ≤ n+1 => a ≤ floor((n+1)/2). So the number of points on y=x in the set is floor((n+1)/2). Similarly, for line y=x-1 (slope 1), points: a = b+1, sum 2b+1 ≤ n+1 => b ≤ floor(n/2). So number = floor(n/2). For line y=x+1, points: a = b-1, sum 2b-1 ≤ n+1 => b ≤ floor((n+2)/2). Actually if y = x + c, then b = a + c. For points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1. So the number of such points is the number of a≥1 such that b = a+c ≥1 and a+b = 2a+c ≤ n+1. So a from max(1, -c+1) to floor((n+1-c)/2). For c=0, a from 1 to floor((n+1)/2). So floor((n+1)/2) points. For c=1, a from 1 to floor((n)/2). So floor(n/2) points. Might be that the maximum number of points on a sunny line (slope 1) is floor((n+1)/2). Similarly, for slope -1 (anti-diagonal) but those are non-sunny. For other slopes, maybe we can have many points if slope is rational. For example, slope 2: points (a,b) with b=2a+c. The condition a,b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1. The number of points would be limited. In general, a line with rational slope can contain many lattice points if it is "dense". The maximum number of lattice points in a triangular region of size n is on the boundary lines (horizontal, vertical, anti-diagonal). For slope 1, we get about n/2. For other slopes, the number is less. So sunny lines can cover many points, but not as many as the best non-sunny lines.

Maybe use different non-sunny lines? For n=5, we need exactly 2 sunny lines. Could we use 2 non-sunny lines that cover all but 3 points, and then 2 sunny lines cover those 3 points? We need to parse problem: A line in the plane is called sunny if it is not parallel to any of the x-axis, the y-axis, or the line x+y=0. So sunny lines are those with slope not 0, not infinite (i.e., not horizontal or vertical), and not slope -1 (since x+y=0 has slope -1). So sunny lines have slopes m such that m ≠ 0, m ≠ ∞, m ≠ -1. So basically all lines except those three directions.

We have n ≥ 3 integer. We need to determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying:
1) For all positive integers a and b with a+b ≤ n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines.
2) Exactly k of the n lines are sunny.

Interpretation: We have a set of n lines (distinct). Condition: For every pair of positive integers a,b such that a+b ≤ n+1, the lattice point (a,b) (with integer coordinates) must lie on at least one of the lines. So we need to cover all such points (a,b) with a,b≥1 integers, a+b ≤ n+1, using n lines. The lines can be any lines (not necessarily through lattice points). We are to find possible k (number of sunny lines among them). So we need to characterize all k for which such a configuration exists.

We need to determine all nonnegative integers k (presumably from 0 to n) such that there exists a set of n distinct lines covering those points, with exactly k sunny lines.

Let's denote S = { (a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1 }. So total number of points is sum_{t=2}^{n+1} (t-1) = (n+1 choose 2) - 1? Actually sum_{s=1}^{n} s = n(n+1)/2, but careful: a+b = t, with t from 2 to n+1. For fixed t, number of positive integer pairs (a,b) with a+b=t is t-1. So total points = sum_{t=2}^{n+1} (t-1) = sum_{j=1}^{n} j = n(n+1)/2. So there are N = n(n+1)/2 points. We have n lines. Each line can cover many points. The condition is that every point is covered by at least one line.

We need to find for which k there exists a set of n lines with exactly k sunny lines covering all those points.

Observation: Lines that are not sunny are those parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0. So there are three families: horizontal lines (y = c), vertical lines (x = c), and lines with slope -1 (x+y = c). Since lines are distinct, we could have some of these.

Let’s think: For a given n, we need to cover all integer points with positive coordinates and sum ≤ n+1. The lines can be placed arbitrarily. Possibly we can use lines that go through many points.

We need to determine all possible k. Likely the answer is all k from 0 to n except maybe some small values. Let's try small n to guess pattern.

n=3. Then points: a,b≥1, a+b≤4. So points: (1,1), (1,2), (2,1), (1,3), (3,1), (2,2). Wait a+b≤4: possibilities: (1,1) sum2; (1,2),(2,1) sum3; (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) sum4. So 6 points. We have 3 lines. We need to cover all 6 points with 3 lines. Can we? Possibly yes. What k? Number of sunny lines among them. We can try to construct.

Line x+y=2 covers (1,1) only. x+y=3 covers (1,2),(2,1)=2 points. x+y=4 covers (1,3),(2,2),(3,1)=3 points. So a line can cover up to 3 points. Since we have 6 points, we need at least 2 lines to cover all points if each covers at most 3. But we have 3 lines, so possible. For example, lines: x=1, y=1, x+y=4? Then x=1 covers (1,1),(1,2),(1,3). y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1). x+y=4 covers (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). But before that, we need to solve problem in general. The problem asks: "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following: for all positive integers a and b with a+b ≤ n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines; and exactly k of the n lines are sunny." So we need to characterize all possible k for a given n≥3.

Observations: The points to cover are lattice points in a triangular region: (a,b) with a,b≥1 integers, a+b ≤ n+1. So there are N = n(n+1)/2 points.

We have n lines. Each line can cover many points. We need to cover all points.

We need to count how many of these lines are "sunny" i.e., not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0. So we need to understand constraints on k.

Potential approach: Use combinatorial geometry, covering problems. Possibly the answer is all k except k=2? But n=3 gave k=0,1,3. n=4 gave k=0,1,3. So k=2 is missing, but also k=4 is missing for n=4. So maybe for each n, the possible k are 0,1, and all k from n-2 to n? But n=3, n-2=1, so k=1, and n=3 is 3, so that would give 0,1,3. For n=4, n-2=2, but k=2 is missing, so not that.

Maybe we need to consider the concept of "sunny lines" and "non-sunny lines". The non-sunny lines are those with slopes 0, ∞, or -1. So they are axis-aligned or anti-diagonal. These lines can cover many points if they pass through many lattice points. The lines with slope 0 (horizontal) cover points with a fixed y. For covering the triangle, the best horizontal line is y=1 covering n points (since a+b≤n+1, a≥1, so if y=1, then a ≤ n). Similarly vertical x=1 covers n points. The anti-diagonal x+y=c for c from 2 to n+1 covers c-1 points. So the maximum number of points on a non-sunny line is n (for c=2? Actually x+y=2 covers 1 point; x+y=n+1 covers n points). So the best non-sunny line covers n points.

We need to cover N points with n lines. This is reminiscent of covering a triangular lattice with lines. Possibly the minimal number of lines needed to cover all points is something like? For n=3, we covered with 3 lines; but maybe 2 lines can't cover all 6 points? Could we cover all 6 points with 2 lines? One line could cover at most 3 points, so 2 lines max 6, but need to cover all 6 with no overlap? Actually two lines could cover 6 points if they are disjoint and each cover 3 points. Is it possible? For n=3, points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1). Could we have two lines covering all? For example, line y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1). Then remaining points: (1,2),(1,3),(2,2). Can a single line cover these three? They are collinear? (1,2) and (2,2) horizontal; (1,3) and (2,2) slope -1; so no. So 2 lines not enough. So 3 lines needed. So n is the minimal number of lines? For n=4, we covered with 4 lines, but could we cover with 3 lines? Possibly? 3 lines max coverage: each line can cover at most 4 points? Actually best non-sunny line covers 4 points, but we need to cover 10 points. With 3 lines, max coverage if they are disjoint is 4+4+4=12, but they can't be all disjoint because points are in triangle; we need to see if 3 lines can cover all 10. Could we use x=1 (4), y=1 (4), and x+y=5 (4)? Their union size is 4+4+4 - overlaps: x=1 and y=1 overlap (1,1); x=1 and x+y=5 overlap (1,4); y=1 and x+y=5 overlap (4,1). So union size = 12 - 3 = 9 points. Missing (2,2),(2,3),(3,2). So not all. Could we use x=1 (4), y=1 (4), and x=2 (3)? Union: x=1:4, y=1:4 overlap (1,1); x=2:3 overlap with x=1? No, x=2 and x=1 disjoint; with y=1? (2,1) overlaps. So distinct: 4+4+3 - overlaps (1,1) and (2,1) = 7+? Actually compute: x=1 covers (1,1)-(1,4). y=1 covers (1,1)-(4,1). x=2 covers (2,1),(2,2),(2,3). Overlap: (1,1) in x=1 and y=1; (2,1) in y=1 and x=2; (1,1) also in x=1 and y=1, no other. So distinct points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(3,1),(4,1) = 9 points. Missing (2,3)? Actually (2,3) is in x=2, so covered; missing (3,2) and (2,2)? (2,2) covered; (3,2) not covered. So missing (3,2). So not all. Could we use y=1 (4), x+y=5 (4), and x=2 (3)? Union: y=1: (1,1)-(4,1); x+y=5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1); x=2: (2,1),(2,2),(2,3). Overlaps: (4,1) in y=1 and x+y=5; (2,3) in x+y=5 and x=2; (2,1) in y=1 and x=2. So distinct: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2) plus (2,2) from x=2. Also (2,3) already, (2,2) new. So total: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(2,2) = 8 points. Missing (1,2),(1,3),(2,2)? (2,2) is there, (1,2) not, (1,3) not. So not all. So 3 lines seem insufficient for n=4. So 4 lines needed. So maybe the minimal number of lines to cover all points is n. And the problem asks: for a given n, we need to use exactly n lines. So we are allowed to use exactly n lines. We want to know possible k (number of sunny lines) among those n lines.

Thus we need to find configurations of n lines that cover all points. Possibly the only possible configurations are those where the non-sunny lines are "full" lines that cover many points, and the sunny lines cover the rest. Might be that for any n, we can achieve k = 0,1, n-1, and n? But n=4 gave k=3 possible, not n=4; n=3 gave k=3 possible. So for n=3, n-1=2, but k=2 not possible. So not that.

Maybe we need to analyze the structure of coverings. Possibly the covering condition forces that the lines must be the three families in a certain way. Let's try to understand necessary conditions.

Let the set of lines be L1,...,Ln. For each point (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1, there exists a line Li such that (a,b) ∈ Li.

We want to maximize/minimize number of sunny lines.

Think of each line as covering a set of points. The triangle is "convex" in lattice. Possibly we can use lines that are "sides" of the triangle. The points lie on a grid. The lines x=1, y=1, x+y=n+1 are "boundary" lines. Those are non-sunny. They cover many points. Could we cover all points using only these three lines? For n=3, union of x=1,y=1,x+y=4 covers all 6 points? Actually we saw x=1 covers (1,1)-(1,4) but (1,4) not in set for n=3 (sum5>4). For n=3, x=1 covers (1,1),(1,2),(1,3); y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1); x+y=4 covers (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). Union = all 6. So for n=3, the three boundary lines cover all points. So k=0 possible. For n=4, union of x=1,y=1,x+y=5 covers 9 points, missing (2,2),(2,3),(3,2). So not all. So we need more lines. So for n=4, we used x=1,x=2,y=1,x+y=5 (4 lines). Could we use only three lines? Probably not. So maybe minimal number of lines needed to cover all points is n. For n=3, 3 lines suffice; for n=4, 4 lines suffice; for n=5, likely 5 lines suffice. So we have n lines, which is minimal. So we need to cover all points with exactly n lines. That imposes constraints: each line must cover many points; maybe the covering is essentially a "partition" of the triangle into n lines each covering a set of points that are "independent" in some sense.

Observation: The number of points is N = n(n+1)/2. If we have n lines, the average number of points per line is N/n = (n+1)/2. So average coverage is about half of n. The maximum coverage by a line is n (if it's a horizontal or vertical or anti-diagonal line that goes through the whole triangle). But if a line covers n points, it might be x=1 (covers a=1..n) or y=1, or x+y=n+1. So those are the best.

We need to cover all points. So likely the covering must include lines that cover the "boundary" points. Perhaps we can classify possible coverings based on the number of non-sunny lines. Since non-sunny lines are the only ones that can cover many points (up to n). Sunny lines cover at most 2 points? Actually we need to check: Could a sunny line cover 3 points? For n=5, points: a,b≥1, a+b≤6. List points: sums 2-6: (1,1); (1,2),(2,1); (1,3),(2,2),(3,1); (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1); (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). That's 1+2+3+4+5=15 points. Can three points be collinear on a line with slope not 0,∞,-1? For example, (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) are on slope 1, but (3,3) has sum6, so it's in the set? For n=5, a+b≤6, so (3,3) sum6 is in the set. So (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) are collinear on y=x. That's slope 1, sunny. So a sunny line can cover 3 points. Similarly, (2,1),(3,2),(4,3) are on y=x-1? slope 1, but (4,3) sum7>6, not in set; (2,1),(3,2) only two. (1,2),(2,3),(3,4) sum? (3,4) sum7 not; (1,2),(2,3) only two. (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) slope -1, not sunny. (2,2),(3,3),(4,4) not; (2,2),(3,3) only two. So there is a triple (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) on y=x. So for n=5, a sunny line can cover 3 points. Could it cover more? (1,1),(2,2),(3,3),(4,4) but (4,4) sum8>6, no. So max 3. For n=6, points sum≤7, triple (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) sum6≤7; (4,4) sum8>7 not; so still 3. For larger n, we can have many points on a line with slope 1 (y=x) as long as a+b≤n+1, so a=b gives 2a ≤ n+1 => a ≤ floor((n+1)/2). So the number of points on y=x in the set is floor((n+1)/2). Similarly, for line y=x-1 (slope 1), points: a = b+1, sum 2b+1 ≤ n+1 => b ≤ floor(n/2). So number = floor(n/2). For line y=x+1, points: a = b-1, sum 2b-1 ≤ n+1 => b ≤ floor((n+2)/2). So number = floor((n+2)/2). Actually careful: For a given slope 1 line, the points with integer coordinates satisfying y = x + c, with c integer, are those with a = b + c? Actually if y = x + c, then b = a + c. For points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1. So the number of such points is the number of a≥1 such that b = a+c ≥1 and a+b = 2a+c ≤ n+1. So a from max(1, -c+1) to floor((n+1-c)/2). For c=0, a from 1 to floor((n+1)/2). So floor((n+1)/2) points. For c=1, a from 1 to floor((n)/2). So floor(n/2) points. For c=-1, a from 2 to floor((n+2)/2) so number = floor((n+2)/2) -1? Actually a≥2 gives number = floor((n+2)/2) -1 = floor((n)/2) for n even? For n=5, c=-1: a from 2 to floor((6)/2)=3 => a=2,3 => 2 points. So that's floor(n/2)? For n=5, floor(5/2)=2. So indeed number = floor((n+1-|c|)/2) - max(0, -c)? Might be that the maximum number of points on a sunny line (slope 1) is floor((n+1)/2). Similarly, for slope -1 (anti-diagonal) but those are non-sunny. For other slopes, maybe we can have many points if slope is rational. For example, slope 2: points (a,b) with b=2a+c. The condition a,b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1. The number of points would be limited. In general, a line with rational slope can contain many lattice points if it is "dense". The maximum number of lattice points in a triangular region of size n is on the boundary lines (horizontal, vertical, anti-diagonal). For slope 1, we get about n/2. For other slopes, the number is less. So sunny lines can cover many points, but not as many as the best non-sunny lines.

Nevertheless, we need to cover all points. The covering might be accomplished by using a set of n lines that are "independent" in some sense. Perhaps there is a known result: The minimal number of lines needed to cover all points of the triangular grid is n, and the only way to do it with n lines is to take all three boundary lines? Thus we need to find a configuration for n=5 that covers all 15 points with 5 lines. Is it possible? Let's try to think systematically. The points form a triangular lattice. So points: (1,1)-(1,5); (2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1); (2,3),(3,2). That's 5+4+2=11? Actually count: x=1:5 points; y=1:4 more (since (1,1) already) so total 9; x+y=5 adds (2,3),(3,2) (4,1 already,1,4 already) so 2 more, total 11. Missing points: (2,2),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,2) maybe? Let's list all points: (1,1)-(1,5); (2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(2,4); (3,1),(3,2),(3,3); (4,1),(4,2); (5,1). So covered: (1,1)-(1,5); (2,1),(2,3); (3,1),(3,2); (4,1); (5,1). Missing: (2,2),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2) = 4 points. So uncovered set size 4. Could 2 sunny lines cover these 4 points? Possibly yes if they can be partitioned into two pairs each on a sunny line. Points: (2,2),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2). Check slopes: (2,2)-(2,4) vertical; (2,2)-(3,3) slope 1, sunny? slope 1 not 0,∞,-1, so sunny; (2,2)-(4,2) horizontal; (2,4)-(3,3) slope -1, not; (2,4)-(4,2) slope (2-4)/(4-2)= -2/2=-1, not; (3,3)-(4,2) slope -1, not; (2,2)-(3,3) sunny; (2,4)-(3,3) slope -1; (2,2)-(4,2) horizontal; (2,4)-(4,2) slope -1; (3,3)-(4,2) slope -1. So the only sunny pair is (2,2)-(3,3). Could we have another sunny pair covering the other two? (2,4) and (4,2) slope -1, no; (2,4) and (3,3) slope -1; (2,2) and (4,2) horizontal; (2,4) and (4,2) slope -1; (2,4) and (2,2) vertical; so no sunny pair for (2,4) and (4,2) together. Could a sunny line cover (2,4) alone? Then (4,2) would need to be covered by the other sunny line possibly with (3,3)? But (3,3)-(4,2) slope -1, not sunny. So (4,2) would need a sunny line alone. So with 2 sunny lines, at most 2 points can be covered if each covers one point, leaving 2 uncovered. But we have 4 uncovered points, so need at least 2 sunny lines covering all 4, which is impossible if each sunny line covers at most 2 and we have 2 lines, they could cover up to 4 if each covers 2. But as we saw, the only sunny pair is (2,2)-(3,3). So we can have one sunny line covering those two. The other sunny line would need to cover (2,4) and (4,2) but they are not on a sunny line together. Could the second sunny line cover (2,4) and (2,2)? That's vertical, no; (2,4) and (3,3) slope -1; (2,4) and (4,2) slope -1; (2,4) and (1,2) horizontal? Not. So no. Could we cover (2,4) and (4,2) with a single sunny line? No. So the only possibility is that the two sunny lines cover at most 2 points each, but they would need to cover 4 points, so they must be disjoint in the uncovered set and each cover exactly 2 points. Since only one pair is sunny, the other two points cannot be on a sunny line together. Could they be on the same sunny line with some other point not in the uncovered set? Possibly the sunny line could pass through one of the uncovered points and also through a point already covered, but that's fine; the line can cover more than just the uncovered points. For example, a sunny line through (2,4) and (2,1) (vertical) not sunny; through (2,4) and (1,3) slope? (3-4)/(1-2)=1/-1=-1? Actually (1,3) to (2,4): slope 1, sunny! So line y=x+2? Check (1,3): 3=1+2; (2,4): 4=2+2; so line y=x+2. That line also passes through (3,5) but not in set; so it covers (2,4) and (1,3) (which is covered already). So that's a sunny line covering (2,4) and also (1,3). That's okay. Similarly, (4,2) could be on a sunny line with (3,1)? (3,1) to (4,2): slope 1, sunny? (2-1)/(4-3)=1, so line y=x-2? At x=3, y=1; x=4, y=2; yes, that's y=x-2. So line y=x-2 covers (4,2) and (3,1). So we could have sunny lines: L1 through (2,2) and (3,3) (y=x), L2 through (2,4) and (1,3) (y=x+2), L3 through (4,2) and (3,1) (y=x-2). That's 3 sunny lines. But we only have 2 sunny lines. Could we combine? Maybe one sunny line covers (2,2)-(3,3) and (2,4)-(1,3) but that would be two different lines; can't be same line because they are not collinear. Could a single sunny line cover (2,2) and (2,4) is vertical, no; (2,2) and (4,2) horizontal; (2,2) and (3,3) already; (2,4) and (4,2) slope -1; (2,4) and (3,3) slope -1; (2,2) and (1,3) slope (3-2)/(1-2)=1/-1=-1; so no. So cannot cover more than 2 points from the uncovered set with a single sunny line. So with 2 sunny lines, at most 4 uncovered points can be covered if each line covers 2 points, but we need exactly the uncovered set of 4 points to be covered. Since only one pair is sunny, the other two points cannot be together on a sunny line, so at most one sunny line can cover 2 points, the other covers at most 1 point, leaving at least 1 uncovered. So 2 sunny lines cannot cover all 4 points. Thus this selection fails.

Maybe use different non-sunny lines? For n=5, we need exactly 2 sunny lines. Could we use 2 non-sunny lines that cover all but 3 points, and then 2 sunny lines cover those 3 points? But as argued, the uncovered points from any two non-sunny lines likely have size 3, and those 3 points might be covered by 2 sunny lines if one of the sunny lines covers two of them and the other covers the third? But we saw that the uncovered set from two non-sunny lines (like x=1,y=1) is {(2,2),(2,3),(3,2)} and no two are on a sunny line. Could we choose non-sunny lines that leave uncovered set of size 3 that includes a sunny pair? For example, if we take x=1 and x=2, uncovered = {(3,1),(3,2),(4,1)}. That set has no sunny pair. If we take y=1 and x=2, uncovered = {(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(3,2),(4,2)}? Actually we computed union of y=1 and x=2 gave 6 points covered, missing 4 points. That's size 4, not 3. So for two non-sunny lines, the uncovered set size is at least 3, and it's exactly 3 when the two lines cover 12 points. But which pairs cover 12? For n=5, points total 15. Two non-sunny lines covering 12 points: possible? The maximum distinct points from two lines is 7 for n=4, but for n=5, what is the maximum? Let's compute. The best non-sunny line covers 5 points (x=1, y=1, x+y=6). Two such lines can cover at most 5+5-1=9 points if they intersect (they share exactly one point). For example, x=1 and y=1 share (1,1) so union size 9. Or x=1 and x+y=6 share (1,5) so union 9. Or y=1 and x+y=6 share (5,1) so union 9. If we take two disjoint non-sunny lines, can they cover more? For example, x=1 (5) and x=2 (4) are disjoint vertical lines, union size 9. So maximum distinct points from 2 non-sunny lines is 9, not 12. For n=5, 2 lines cover at most 9 points, leaving at least 6 points uncovered. That's too many for 2 sunny lines to cover? Could 2 sunny lines cover 6 points? Possibly if each covers 3 points on a sunny line, but we need to see if there exist 3 points collinear on a sunny line. As we saw, for n=5, there is a triple (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) on y=x. So a sunny line can cover 3 points. Could it cover more? Possibly (1,2),(2,3),(3,4) but (3,4) not in set; (2,1),(3,2),(4,3) no; (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) slope -1, not sunny. So the maximum coverage by a sunny line is 3 (for n=5, y=x gives 3 points). Could a sunny line cover 4 points? For n=5, the points on y=x are 1,1;2,2;3,3. That's 3. For y=x+1: (1,2),(2,3) only 2; y=x-1: (2,1),(3,2) only 2; so max 3. So with 2 sunny lines, max coverage = 3+3=6. So if 2 non-sunny lines cover 9 points, leaving 6 uncovered, it's possible that 2 sunny lines cover those 6 points, provided each sunny line covers exactly 3 points from that uncovered set and they are disjoint. So we need to see if there exists a selection of 2 non-sunny lines such that the uncovered set (the 6 points) can be partitioned into two subsets each of size 3 that are collinear on sunny lines. That would give k=2.

Thus we need to examine all possible pairs of non-sunny lines for n=5, see if the uncovered set can be covered by 2 sunny lines.

First, note that the points are: (1,1); (1,2),(2,1); (1,3),(2,2),(3,1); (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1); (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). So there are 5 "layers" of constant sum: sum2: (1,1); sum3: (1,2),(2,1); sum4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1); sum5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1); sum6: (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). 

We need 2 non-sunny lines. The possible lines are:
- Horizontal y=c: c=1..5. y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1) =5 points. y=2 covers (1,2),(2,2),(3,2),(4,2) =4 points. y=3 covers (1,3),(2,3),(3,3) =3 points. y=4 covers (1,4),(2,4) =2 points. y=5 covers (1,5) =1 point.

So not sunny. So no triple on a sunny line. Thus each sunny line covers at most 2 points. With 2 sunny lines, max coverage 4, but we have 6 points, impossible. So (x=1,x+y=6) fails.

- (x=2, x=3): x=2 covers 4 points, x=3 covers 3 points, disjoint? x=2: (2,1)-(2,4); x=3: (3,1)-(3,3). Union size = 7. That leaves 8 uncovered. But we need 2 sunny lines to cover 8 points, which is possible if each sunny line covers 4 points. Are there 4 points collinear on a sunny line? For n=5, max per sunny line is 3, as we saw. So 2 sunny lines max 6, cannot cover 8. So any pair that covers only 7 points leaves 8 uncovered, which is too many for 2 sunny lines. So we need pairs that cover 9 points (max). The only pairs that cover 9 points are those with both lines covering 5 points and intersecting (or disjoint with 5+4=9? Actually x=1 and x=2 give 5+4=9, but that's 9. x=1 and y=1 give 5+5-1=9. x=1 and x+y=6 give 5+5-1=9. y=1 and x+y=6 give 5+5-1=9. Also possibly x=1 and x=3? 5+3=8, not 9. So the only pairs with union 9 are those. So we have tested all: (x=1,x=2), (x=1,y=1), (x=1,x+y=6), (y=1,x+y=6). In each case, uncovered set size 6, and we argued no 2 sunny lines can cover them because each sunny line covers at most 2 points (max 3 but no triple on sunny line). But we need to be thorough: maybe there is a sunny line that covers 3 points from the uncovered set for some U? For (x=1,x=2), U = {(3,1),(3,2),(3,3),(4,1),(4,2),(5,1)}. Check if any triple is collinear on a sunny line. The only possible collinear triples: (3,1),(4,1),(5,1) horizontal y=1 (non-sunny); (3,2),(4,2) only 2; (3,3) alone; (3,1),(3,2),(3,3) vertical; (4,1),(4,2) vertical; (5,1) alone. So no triple on sunny line. So max 2.

For (x=1,y=1), U = {(2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(3,2),(3,3),(4,2)}. Check triples: (2,2),(3,2),(4,2) horizontal y=2 (non-sunny); (2,2),(2,3),(2,4) vertical; (2,2),(2,3),(3,2) no; (2,2),(2,3),(3,3) no; (2,2),(2,4),(3,3) no; (2,3),(3,2),(4,1) not; (2,3),(3,3),(4,2) slope? (3-2)/(2-3)=? Actually (2,3) to (3,3) horizontal; (2,3) to (4,2) slope -1/2; not same. (2,4),(3,3),(4,2) slope -1? (3-4)/(3-2)=-1; (2-3)/(4-3)=-1; so they are collinear on x+y=6? 2+4=6, 3+3=6, 4+2=6, so that's non-sunny. So no triple on sunny line. So max 2.

We were unable to produce a complete proof. However, the strongest partial progress is as follows:
- We constructed configurations for n=3 and n=4, showing possible k values.
- We attempted to find a pattern for general n, but we were not able to complete the proof.
- The problem likely expects a characterization of all k that can occur, possibly all k except 2? But we need a rigorous proof.

Given the complexity, we might attempt to prove that k can be any integer from 0 to n except 2? But we saw for n=3, k=2 is impossible, and for n=4, k=2 is impossible, and also k=4 is impossible. So not that.

Maybe we can prove that the only possible values are 0,1,n-1,n? But n=3 gave 0,1,3, which is 0,1,n, and n=4 gave 0,1,3, which is 0,1,n-1. So 0,1 are always possible, and sometimes n, n-1, etc.

Maybe we can prove that k can be any integer except 2? But we need to verify for n=5: we need to see if k=2 is possible, and also k=5? We need to produce a configuration for n=5 with exactly 2 sunny lines, and also with exactly 5 sunny lines? If k=5 is possible, then all k from 0 to 5 except 2? That would be 0,1,3,4,5. But we need to check if k=5 is possible: can we have 5 lines all sunny covering all points? For n=5, points total 15. If we have 5 sunny lines, each line can cover at most 3 points (as we saw, no triple on a sunny line for n=5? Actually for n=5, we have points on y=x: (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) - that's 3 points, so a sunny line can cover 3 points. With 5 lines, max coverage 15, so possible if each line covers exactly 3 points and they are disjoint. Is there a partition of the 15 points into 5 triples each collinear on a line with slope not 0,∞,-1? This would require 5 lines each containing 3 points from the set, and they must be distinct. The points are lattice points in a triangle. Possibly we can take lines: y=x (covers (1,1),(2,2),(3,3)), y=x-1 (covers (2,1),(3,2) only 2 points), not enough. So maybe not. So k=5 might be impossible for n=5. So pattern not that.

Given the difficulty, it might be that the only possible k are 0,1, and n-1? For n=3, n-1=2, but 2 is impossible, so not.

Maybe the answer is: all k except k=2? But we need to check n=3,4,5,... Could we construct for n=5 with k=2? Possibly. We need to produce a configuration of 5 lines covering all 15 points, with exactly 2 sunny lines. This might be possible. We attempted to find a configuration using non-sunny lines that cover 12 points, leaving 3 points to be covered by 2 sunny lines, but we saw that the 3 points from the uncovered set cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines because no two are on a sunny line? Actually we need to cover those 3 points with 2 sunny lines; one line could cover 2 of them and the other covers the third. But we saw that the uncovered set from a pair of non-sunny lines was such that no two are on a sunny line. So that fails. But we could use a different selection: maybe use 3 non-sunny lines covering 14 points, leaving 1 point to be covered by 2 sunny lines? That would be overkill. So we need to cover all points with 5 lines, where we have 2 sunny lines. That means the 3 non-sunny lines must cover all points, but they can also be sunny? No, they are non-sunny. So the 3 non-sunny lines must cover the entire set, because the 2 sunny lines can cover additional points but the non-sunny lines must cover all points. Actually the condition is that each point lies on at least one of the lines. So if the non-sunny lines cover all points, then the sunny lines are redundant. So we can have a configuration where the 3 non-sunny lines already cover all points. For n=5, is there a set of 3 non-sunny lines that cover all 15 points? Possibly x=1, y=1, x+y=6? Their union covers 9 points, missing 6. So not. x=1, x=2, y=1? Union covers 12 points, missing 3. So not. x=1, x=2, x=3? Union covers 12 points, missing 3. So 3 non-sunny lines cannot cover all 15 points. So we need the 2 sunny lines to cover some points not covered by the non-sunny lines. So we need to find 3 non-sunny lines and 2 sunny lines such that together they cover all points. The 3 non-sunny lines will cover some set, and the sunny lines cover the rest. This is more flexible.

We could try to use x=1, y=1, and x=2? That's 3 non-sunny lines: x=1 (5 points), y=1 (5 points, overlap (1,1)), x=2 (4 points, overlap with y=1 at (2,1), with x=1? no). Union of these three: x=1: (1,1)-(1,5); y=1: (1,1)-(5,1); x=2: (2,1)-(2,4). Overlaps: (1,1) in x=1 and y=1; (2,1) in y=1 and x=2; (1,1) also in x=1 and y=1. So distinct points: x=1:5; y=1 adds (2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1) but (1,1) already, so +4; x=2 adds (2,2),(2,3),(2,4) but (2,1) already, so +3. Total = 5+4+3=12 points. Missing: (3,2),(3,3),(4,2) maybe? Let's list all points: (1,1)-(1,5); (2,1)-(2,4); (3,1)-(3,3); (4,1)-(4,2); (5,1). Covered: (1,1)-(1,5) all; (2,1) covered; (2,2) covered; (2,3) covered; (2,4) covered; (3,1) covered; (4,1) covered; (5,1) covered. Missing: (3,2),(3,3),(4,2) = 3 points. So uncovered set U = {(3,2),(3,3),(4,2)}. Now we have 2 sunny lines to cover these 3 points. Check if any two are on a sunny line: (3,2)-(3,3) vertical, not; (3,2)-(4,2) horizontal, not; (3,3)-(4,2) slope (2-3)/(4-3)=-1, not. So no pair is on a sunny line. Therefore, with 2 sunny lines, at most 2 points can be covered (if each covers one point) but we need to cover 3 points, impossible. So that selection fails.
